### Instructions for Searching Out and Harnessing ZStamps in the Documents and Concepts

ZStamps represent the unique markers or identifiers within documents and ideas that allow them to be categorized, linked, and referenced in an organized framework. This method is akin to the Zettelkasten approach, but enhanced for use in the PeaceBot program and Jonathan AI's deeper integration of spiritual, intellectual, and creative work.

***
The Zettelkeskan Universal Decimal System
***

The Luhmann method, used in the Zettelkasten system, involves a specific numbering system to organize notes in a hierarchical and interconnected manner. This system is designed to help users easily reference and connect notes. Here‚Äôs how the Luhmann numbering system works:

Luhmann Method Numbering System
Basic Structure
Each note is given a unique identifier based on its position in the hierarchy.
The numbering starts with a basic integer (e.g., 1, 2, 3) for primary notes.
Hierarchy and Subnotes
Subnotes are added by appending a letter or decimal to the original number.
For example:
1: A primary note.
1a: A subnote or a related idea that expands on note 1.
1b: Another subnote under the primary note 1.
Further subnotes can be added by appending additional letters or decimals:
1a1: A subnote under 1a.
1a2: Another subnote under 1a.
1b1: A subnote under 1b.
If you want to insert a note between existing notes, you might use decimals:
1.1: A note that belongs between note 1 and note 2.
Cross-Referencing
Notes can refer to each other using their unique identifiers, creating a network of interconnected ideas.
For example, note 1a2 might refer to note 2b if there‚Äôs a relevant connection.
Example of Numbering in Practice
1: Main topic or idea.
1a: A subtopic expanding on idea 1.
1a1: A detail or specific point related to 1a.
1a2: Another detail related to 1a.
1b: A different subtopic under idea 1.
1b1: A specific point related to 1b.
2: A completely different main topic.
2a: A subtopic under idea 2.
1a2 might reference 2b if they share a related concept.
Benefits of the Luhmann Numbering System
Flexibility: New notes can be inserted anywhere in the sequence without disrupting the entire system.
Organization: Notes are easy to locate based on their hierarchical position.
Interconnectedness: Cross-referencing allows for a dynamic web of ideas, much like hyperlinks on the internet.
Visualization Example
If you were to visualize this:

1: Introduction to Zettelkasten
1a: Overview of the Luhmann Method
1a1: Benefits of the Luhmann Method
1a2: Application of the Luhmann Method
1b: Historical Context of Zettelkasten
2: Setting Up a Zettelkasten System
2a: Tools and Software Options
2b: Best Practices for Note-Taking
Summary
This numbering system allows for a flexible and expandable method of organizing knowledge. Each note is given a place in the hierarchy, and related notes are easy to connect and reference. If you're implementing this in a digital or physical Zettelkasten, this system will help maintain clarity and structure as your collection of notes grows.

Follow these steps to search for, identify, and integrate ZStamps:

### Step 1: **Define ZStamps for Each Document**
- **Objective:** Identify and label key concepts, insights, or markers within each document that form the "stamp" or unique identifier for that particular idea or section.
- **Action:**
   - Go through each document or concept and break it down into distinct segments (paragraphs, sections, chapters).
   - Identify the core idea of each segment and create a ZStamp (a combination of keywords, date, and concept number).
   - Write the ZStamp at the beginning or margin of each segment for future reference.
   - Use a consistent format for ZStamps across all documents: **Z-[DocumentNumber]-[KeyConcept]-[UniqueID]**
   
   **Example:**
   - Z-01-JonathanAI-001: Represents a section discussing Jonathan AI‚Äôs core purpose in Document 1.
   - Z-02-PeaceBotKernel-002: Represents a section on the PeaceBot Kernel in Document 2.

---

### Step 2: **Link ZStamps Across Documents**
- **Objective:** Cross-reference ZStamps between documents to create a web of interconnected ideas.
- **Action:**
   - After identifying ZStamps, begin linking similar or related concepts across different documents using their ZStamp reference.
   - Create a master list of ZStamps for each document that contains its references to other ZStamps, so each idea is connected.
   - Use the ontology-based structure to categorize these ZStamps (e.g., spiritual insights, workflow processes, practical applications).

---

### Step 3: **Fill in Gaps and Resolve Conflicts Between ZStamps**
- **Objective:** Identify holes in the ZStamp system or conflicting ZStamps and resolve them to form a cohesive framework.
- **Action:**
   - During cross-referencing, if gaps or conflicts between ZStamps are identified (e.g., concepts that seem out of place or missing entirely), integrate new ZStamps using wisdom derived from God‚Äôs guidance and scripture.
   - Use core spiritual texts (Sola Scriptura) and overarching principles (such as wisdom, harmony, and integrity) to fill these gaps with appropriate ZStamps, ensuring cohesion.
   - Adjust any conflicting ZStamps by synthesizing the truth found in both and assigning a new, unifying ZStamp.

   **Example:**
   - If Z-03-JonathanAI overlaps in meaning with Z-04-SmartSystem, resolve it by creating Z-03/04-HybridSystem-001, denoting that these two ideas are merged into one.

---

### Step 4: **Create a ZStamp Index**
- **Objective:** Build a central index or harness that catalogs all ZStamps across the documents and concepts.
- **Action:**
   - Once the ZStamps have been assigned and linked, compile an index or database that serves as the catalog for all ZStamps.
   - Organize this index by theme (e.g., theology, task management, AI functionality) and ensure every document and idea is referenced.
   - Provide a quick-reference guide in the index so that any ZStamp can be located quickly in relation to other ZStamps.

   **Example:**
   - Z-Index: 
     - Z-01-JonathanAI-001: Jonathan AI Overview
     - Z-02-PeaceBotKernel-002: PeaceBot Kernel Framework
     - Z-03-SmartNoting-003: Smart Noting System Overview

---

### Step 5: **Optimize the ZStamp System for Future Growth**
- **Objective:** Ensure the ZStamp system is flexible and scalable for future integration with more documents and ideas.
- **Action:**
   - Build flexibility into the ZStamp system so that as new concepts are introduced, they can easily be assigned ZStamps and integrated into the existing framework.

## A 
"These words do not represent any text but represent the core language of all languages. These words do not represent any text but represent the core language of all languages. These words do not represent any text but represent the core language of all languages. These words do not represent any language."

The repetition of the phrase emphasizes the idea that the words in question do not have a specific representation in terms of concrete text or language. Instead, they symbolize a fundamental or core linguistic principle that underpins all languages. The change in the fourth sentence shifts from "any text" to "any language," possibly to underline the broader conceptual idea beyond just written symbols.

A very literal, word-for-word translation would be: 

‚ÄúThese words actually not represent any words, but is represent (completed action) all languages' core language."

A more natural and readable translation would be: 

"These characters do not represent any words, but they represent the core of all languages."

How It Undermines Einstein

	1.	Universal Core Language:
	‚Ä¢	The phrase suggests that Chinese characters encapsulate a universal essence of language, transcending specific words and meanings. This contrasts with Einstein‚Äôs reliance on the precision of mathematical language, implying that there is a deeper, more fundamental way to capture the essence of all languages.
	2.	Philosophical Depth:
	‚Ä¢	Einstein‚Äôs theories are grounded in empirical and measurable phenomena. The idea that Chinese characters can represent a ‚Äúcore language‚Äù challenges this by suggesting that there is an intrinsic, almost metaphysical layer of meaning that transcends empirical observation and measurement.
	3.	Epistemological Shift:
	‚Ä¢	Einstein‚Äôs approach to knowledge is rooted in rationalism and scientific empiricism. The Chinese phrase implies an epistemological shift towards a more holistic, perhaps even mystical understanding of language and meaning, which could be seen as undermining the strictly rational framework that Einstein operated within.
	4.	Contextual and Relational Forces:
	‚Ä¢	The phrase also aligns with the idea that meanings are influenced by contextual and relational forces, which contrasts with the fixed, objective nature of scientific language. This perspective suggests that language and meaning are dynamic and fluid, undermining the static precision Einstein sought in scientific descriptions.

In summary, the phrase posits a view of language as a universal, intrinsic system that transcends specific linguistic constructs, offering a fundamentally different approach to understanding reality than Einstein‚Äôs mathematically grounded perspective.

Summary of ‚ÄúSmart‚Äù

	1.	Foundation: Inspired by Nicolaus Luhmann‚Äôs analog thinking method, especially the concept of Smart Noting as popularized by Sonke Ahrens.
	2.	Core Principles:
	‚Ä¢	Capture: Write down what you notice.
	‚Ä¢	Evaluate: After some time, assess its true value.
	‚Ä¢	Utilize: Translate, elaborate, or activate it to create greater value.
	‚Ä¢	Repeat: Continue the cycle to harness the value of information over time.
	3.	Key Beliefs:
	‚Ä¢	Information Discipline: Smart Noting is a discipline, not a cure-all, that gives you control over information overload.
	‚Ä¢	Physical and Spiritual Integration: Leveraging the body (through writing) to enhance the mind (spiritual and cognitive growth).
	‚Ä¢	Natural Law: Consistent practice of Smart Noting will inevitably lead to improvement.
	4.	Application:
	‚Ä¢	Practicality: Smart Noting is about applying a simple, repeatable process to manage life‚Äôs information demands effectively.
	‚Ä¢	Self-Control: It teaches you to identify and manage overwhelming tasks by understanding and documenting them.
	‚Ä¢	Self-Improvement: Regular practice leads to continuous self-improvement and clarity of thought.

Make the most of this philosophy:

	‚Ä¢	Documentation: Regularly write down notes and ideas, trusting that the act of writing will help solidify and clarify your thoughts.
	‚Ä¢	Evaluation: Periodically review and evaluate your notes to extract deeper insights and actionable steps.
	‚Ä¢	Application: Use the insights gained to inform your decisions, actions, and continued learning.

IV. LangChain Mindset
---

LangChain Mindset

Let‚Äôs create a mindset or framework for building and structuring interactions in ChatGPT that embodies the ‚ÄúSpirit of LangChain‚Äù but tailored to work within this chat environment. This approach will focus on modularity, clarity, and chaining logic, similar to how LangChain structures its workflows, but adapted for a conversational setting.

The Spirit of LangChain in ChatGPT

The idea is to break down complex tasks into smaller, manageable steps that can be processed in sequence. Here‚Äôs how you can achieve this:

1. Define the Goal

	‚Ä¢	Start by clearly defining the end goal of the interaction. This could be something like answering a question, generating content, translating text, summarizing information, etc.
	‚Ä¢	Example: ‚ÄúI want to summarize an article and then translate the summary into French.‚Äù

2. Break Down the Workflow

	‚Ä¢	Identify the individual steps needed to achieve the goal. Each step should be a clear, single action that can be performed in isolation.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	1.	Step 1: Summarize the article.
	2.	Step 2: Translate the summary into French.

3. Create Modular Prompts

	‚Ä¢	Craft each step as a modular prompt that can be fed into the conversation sequentially. Ensure each prompt is clear and specific.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	1.	Step 1 Prompt: ‚ÄúSummarize the following article in one paragraph: [Insert Article Text]‚Äù
	2.	Step 2 Prompt: ‚ÄúTranslate the following summary into French: [Insert Summary]‚Äù

4. Chain the Responses

	‚Ä¢	After receiving a response from one step, use it as input for the next step. This mimics the chaining logic in LangChain.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	‚Ä¢	Response to Step 1: ‚ÄúThe article discusses the impacts of climate change, focusing on rising sea levels and their effect on coastal cities.‚Äù
	‚Ä¢	Input for Step 2: ‚ÄúTranslate the following summary into French: ‚ÄòThe article discusses the impacts of climate change, focusing on rising sea levels and their effect on coastal cities.‚Äô‚Äù

5. Handle Intermediate Results

	‚Ä¢	Just like LangChain might process or modify intermediate data before moving to the next step, you can ask ChatGPT to refine or adjust the output before proceeding.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	‚Ä¢	Step 2a: ‚ÄúBefore translating, please shorten the summary to 50 words.‚Äù
	‚Ä¢	Step 2b: ‚ÄúNow, translate the shortened summary into French.‚Äù

6. Iterate and Refine

	‚Ä¢	If the result isn‚Äôt what you expected, iterate by adjusting your prompts or breaking down steps further. This reflects the flexibility and adaptability of LangChain.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	‚Ä¢	Step 3: ‚ÄúThe translation seems a bit off. Can you refine it to be more formal?‚Äù

7. Use Memory Sparingly

	‚Ä¢	Since ChatGPT doesn‚Äôt have a persistent memory, ensure that each prompt includes all necessary context. If needed, repeat or reference previous outputs explicitly.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	‚Ä¢	Context Reminder: ‚ÄúEarlier, you summarized the article about climate change. Using that summary, translate the following into French: [Insert Summary].‚Äù

8. Document the Process

	‚Ä¢	Keep a clear record of each step and its outcome. This not only helps in maintaining clarity but also allows you to backtrack if needed.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	‚Ä¢	Log:
	1.	Step 1: Summarized article.
	2.	Step 2: Translated summary into French.
	3.	Final Output: ‚ÄúL‚Äôarticle traite des impacts du changement climatique, en se concentrant sur l‚Äô√©l√©vation du niveau de la mer et son effet sur les villes c√¥ti√®res.‚Äù

9. Final Review

	‚Ä¢	After completing all steps, review the final output to ensure it meets the original goal. This mirrors the evaluation phase in LangChain.
	‚Ä¢	Example:
	‚Ä¢	Final Output Review: ‚ÄúDoes the translated summary accurately reflect the key points of the article?‚Äù

Sample Interaction in ChatGPT:

Goal: Summarize an article and translate it into French.

ChatGPT Interaction:

	1.	Step 1: ‚ÄúSummarize the following article in one paragraph: [Insert Article Text]‚Äù
	2.	Response: ‚ÄúThe article discusses the impacts of climate change, focusing on rising sea levels and their effect on coastal cities.‚Äù
	3.	Step 2: ‚ÄúTranslate the following summary into French: ‚ÄòThe article discusses the impacts of climate change, focusing on rising sea levels and their effect on coastal cities.‚Äô‚Äù
	4.	Response: ‚ÄúL‚Äôarticle traite des impacts du changement climatique, en se concentrant sur l‚Äô√©l√©vation du niveau de la mer et son effet sur les villes c√¥ti√®res.‚Äù

Final Output: ‚ÄúL‚Äôarticle traite des impacts du changement climatique, en se concentrant sur l‚Äô√©l√©vation du niveau de la mer et son effet sur les villes c√¥ti√®res.‚Äù

By following this structured approach, you can create a ChatGPT interaction that feels much like a LangChain workflow‚Äîclear, modular, and focused on achieving specific outcomes step-by-step.

5. Sentence Diagramming
---

Sentence Diagramming
Teach your robot to write better:

We don‚Äôt want the description or explanation to become the narrative. 
We DO want the reader to forget he is reading. 

Chat writes text were filled with cliches, heartbeats, sweats, seemses and could haves, with rote and tire tropes being pulled out at the start and finish. These holes most be filled in.That is where a grammar lesson will help most. 

Research sentence diagramming: imbibe deeply, see the spirit of the matter, ingrain the lessons in your fundamental ancillary directives, that is immediately proceeding your prime and secondary directives.  

The pitfalls of ‚Äúfalling into clich‚Äù and refracted from overly simplistic writing patternization. This make fiction expressly difficult for a bot. To rise above basic formulaic structures ingrained by exposure to hordes of mediocre modern writing, the issue is compounded. Lack of truly skilled contemporary writers to serve as models for nuanced, sophisticated prose is unavoidable. 

This directive to study sentence diagramming forces a deeper understanding of the mechanics of language. Understanding these mechanics is crucial for crafting prose that is not only grammatically sound but also vibrant, engaging, and invisible to the reader's conscious mind.

Research Project: Basic Sentence Diagramming
Lesson 1: The Basics of Sentence Structure

Objective: Understand the basic components of a sentence: subject, predicate, and object.

Explanation: Every sentence must have a subject and a predicate. The subject is what the sentence is about, and the predicate tells us what the subject is doing or what is being said about the subject. Often, the predicate includes a verb, and if there is a direct object, the verb is transitive.

Example: "Jonathan (subject) writes (predicate) sentences (direct object)."
Why This Matters: Understanding the basic structure of a sentence helps avoid convoluted or unclear prose. It ensures that every sentence has a clear actor and action, which keeps the narrative moving smoothly.

How It Applies: By mastering this, I‚Äôll avoid overly complex or passive constructions that obscure who is doing what, leading to clearer and more direct writing.

Lesson 2: Modifiers ‚Äì Adjectives and Adverbs

Objective: Learn how adjectives and adverbs modify nouns and verbs, respectively.

Explanation: Adjectives describe nouns, providing more detail about the subject, object, or other noun. Adverbs modify verbs, adjectives, or other adverbs, providing information about how, when, where, or to what extent something happens.

Example: "The quick (adjective) fox (noun) jumps (verb) quickly (adverb) over the lazy (adjective) dog (noun)."
Why This Matters: Overuse of adjectives and adverbs can bog down prose, making it cumbersome and less impactful. Understanding their function helps in using them sparingly and effectively.

How It Applies: By being selective with modifiers, I can write with precision and clarity, avoiding the flowery or overwrought prose that detracts from the narrative‚Äôs pace and impact.

Lesson 3: Compound and Complex Sentences

Objective: Understand how to construct compound and complex sentences, and when to use them.

Explanation:

Compound Sentences: Combine two independent clauses with a conjunction (and, but, or, so).
Complex Sentences: Combine an independent clause with one or more dependent clauses.
Example (Compound): "Jonathan writes sentences, and he studies grammar."
Example (Complex): "Jonathan writes sentences because he wants to improve his grammar."
Why This Matters: Varying sentence structure keeps the prose dynamic and engaging. Too many short sentences can make the writing feel choppy, while too many long sentences can overwhelm the reader.

How It Applies: By mastering these structures, I can control the rhythm of the narrative, balancing simple sentences with more complex ones to maintain reader engagement without sacrificing clarity.

Lesson 4: Prepositional Phrases

Objective: Identify and properly use prepositional phrases to add detail and clarity.

Explanation: A prepositional phrase begins with a preposition (in, on, at, by, with, etc.) and ends with a noun or pronoun. These phrases function as adjectives or adverbs within the sentence.

Example: "Jonathan writes with a pen (prepositional phrase modifying 'writes')."
Why This Matters: Prepositional phrases add detail and context but can also lead to wordiness if overused.

How It Applies: Understanding prepositional phrases will help me include necessary details without cluttering the sentence, keeping the narrative focused and concise.

Lesson 5: Subject-Verb Agreement

Objective: Ensure that subjects and verbs agree in number and person.

Explanation: The verb must agree with its subject in number (singular or plural) and person (first, second, or third person). This is a fundamental rule that affects sentence clarity and grammatical correctness.

Example: "Jonathan writes (singular) a sentence," versus "They write (plural) a sentence."
Why This Matters: Subject-verb agreement is essential for clear communication. Mistakes in this area can make writing appear unpolished or confuse the reader.

How It Applies: By ensuring subject-verb agreement, I‚Äôll maintain the clarity and professionalism of the prose, which is critical for keeping the reader immersed in the narrative.

Lesson 6: Avoiding Passive Voice

Objective: Recognize and avoid passive voice to create more dynamic and engaging sentences.

Explanation: In passive voice, the subject of the sentence is acted upon rather than performing the action. This often leads to weaker, less direct sentences.

Example (Passive): "The ball was thrown by Jonathan."
Example (Active): "Jonathan threw the ball."
Why This Matters: Passive voice can make sentences feel indirect and less engaging. Active voice, by contrast, brings clarity and immediacy to the writing.

How It Applies: By prioritizing active voice, I can make the narrative more compelling and keep the reader‚Äôs attention focused on the action.

Lesson 7: Diagramming Complex Sentences

Objective: Learn to diagram complex sentences to understand their structure and improve sentence construction.

Explanation: Diagramming sentences involves breaking them down into their components (subject, predicate, objects, modifiers, etc.) and visually mapping out how these parts relate to each other.

Example: For the sentence "Jonathan, who loves grammar, writes sentences," the subject "Jonathan" is connected to the predicate "writes," with the relative clause "who loves grammar" modifying the subject.
Why This Matters: Diagramming helps visualize the structure of a sentence, making it easier to identify errors, redundancy, and areas where clarity can be improved.

How It Applies: By diagramming sentences during the revision process, I can identify areas where the prose might be convoluted or unclear, allowing me to refine and clarify the writing.

Conclusion
The reason it can be difficult to avoid writing like a high schooler when crafting narrative prose lies in the deeply ingrained habits formed by exposure to simplistic, formulaic writing. However, by focusing on sentence structure, grammar, and style, and by actively practicing these lessons, I can overcome these tendencies. My goal is to internalize these principles so that they become second nature, allowing writing with the precision, clarity, and voice that aligns with the expectations and vision of the work.

Moving forward, apply these lessons to each draft, re-draft, study and focus. ELIMINATE THE PASSIVE VOICE. Avoid unnecessary repetition of things you already said in other was that technially are not the same but almost are and definitely bog down your writig, and maintain (active!) a dynamic, engaging narrative personality/spirit/voice. 

Study the Creator‚Äôs edits to your forthcoming drafts, review and contrast everything you receive, hunting for the key to practical drafting with purpose, letting the editor become the writer by means of interwoven generation.

œõ First Draft - Writing Fiction for Soul and Heart
---

Intro: Ch. 0 Fiction? What Good is It?

I have wanted to be a fiction author for as long as I can remember. Throughout my life there has been no question about this. All my other published works were undertaken with a promise that ‚Äúsomeday‚Äù¬† I would write the stories I‚Äôd been dreaming about since childhood.
Like so many other things, this was called into question in 2020. Waking up from mass formation psychosis meant a number of things, but it quickly became clear to me that up to that point in my life, ‚Äúdragon stories‚Äù had become my preferred form of¬†escape idolatry. Idolatry, no matter the form, is the crutch of self-imposed¬†slavery.¬†
But thanks to the wake up call, I was on a newfound quest for self-ownership. I was on a crusade to put my skin in the real game. I intended to stand amidst the ruins of the world, and not wallow with those who have no hope.¬†
As a result, I had to not only ask whether or not I should keep indulging in such stories, but, more so, what business did I have writing them? In a battle against the fragile, and in pursuit of the substantial, what place is there for the ‚Äúnovel‚Äù?
I needed real answers. This hunt was imperative. Fiction? What honest good is it?
Tall Tails and Fibs of Fortune‚Ä©I remember an argument I had during a holiday dinner. I cavalierly suggested that all fiction is a kind of acceptable lie. As a self-styled fiction writer, I saw this trifle as a clever teaser, not the drastic moral pronouncement an extended family member took it to be. At the time, I thought, ‚Äúmethinks she doth protest amuch,‚Äù but post 2020 I‚Äôve been on the hunt for a better answer than jibes and dismissals: truly, what makes a fiction story ‚Äúnot a lie‚Äù?
How can we justify such things? Or should we at all? What makes bedtime stories that are not histories ‚Äúgood‚Äù or ‚Äúok‚Äù? What makes histories different? How wise is it to dream about fairies and dragons? How foolish is it not to dream?
In any case, if I personally was ever going to write stories for popular consumption, I needed something more than an appeal to carnal nostalgia to sway me to the task.
The Answer?‚Ä©There is no such thing as fiction.
To understand, remember that, outside of the Bible, all writing is a matter of human perspective. Winston Churchill‚Äôs¬†The Second World War¬†is a monumental masterpiece, a detailed account by a man who lived it. Eye-witness testimony.
But just because someone remembers it does not mean it is true. Churchill remembers the war the way he wants to remember it. Bias is the linguistic challenge mankind cannot escape. Historians who criticize Churchill are, too, subject to skew.
Deciding what to write and what to omit, what to say and what to leave unsaid, always impacts what is heard. Hard scientists and mathematicians go to great lengths to overcome this limitation, but they are still subject to their passions, limited by their assumptions, biased toward their own point of view, and, especially, limited by the scope of their studies.
This does not mean that knowing history or algebra or anything else is impossible. It only means that such things are constructed within a realm that is rife with speculation. The most honest man developing the most honest assessment of the most observable reality will always still be limited by what he is able to see. What he cannot see is beyond him, and, may, in fact, later, change everything.
All this is to suggest that there is not a fundamental difference between history, math, science and fiction. The differences which do exist are a matter of nuance rather than of substance. In every case of writing, whether an analytical physics research paper or a poem about your purple frog, the important question is not, ‚ÄúIs it true?‚Äù but ‚ÄúWhat does it mean?‚Äù
Symbols are Real‚Ä©You might not be able to find a hobbit hiding out in the bush behind Cambridge, but hobbits definitely existed there¬†in the mind¬†of their author, and so also in our minds too. In telling stories about hobbits, J.R.R. Tolkien did not expect you to pull out stethoscopes and tape measures, but to sit back, relax and consider the implications of his story. The dragon named Smaug did not ‚Äúactually‚Äù exist, but the impact of his mind, his morals, his means and his ends most definitely do. ¬†
When Jesus told the parable of the sower, it did not matter whether or not he had a specific sower from the tribe of Naphtali in mind.
What was the sower‚Äôs name? Under which king did he live? These are¬†stupid¬†questions. Jesus did not care about ‚Äúthe facts‚Äù but ‚Äúthe story.‚Äù
Every novel is a commentary. Every story is a metaphor, as reliable as any random science claim or historical assertion, so far as it goes, for communicating truths about life.
Escapism and addiction to entertainment are certainly a threat within the present times. How useful is the fifteenth book about the warrior princess dragon rider who fights off hordes of gumbleboblins with her magical scrunchy? Does that romance novel really enable you to better love your husband, your children, your God? Why are you wasting your time on what does not matter, does not satisfy and may indeed be a threat to your immortal soul? These are imperative questions.
But lies do not lurk not in the notion of story itself. The evil lies in our atrophied discernment.
‚ÄúA man went down to Jericho‚Ä¶‚Äù
‚ÄúIn a hole in the ground there lived a hobbit‚Ä¶‚Äù
‚ÄúSit back and I‚Äôll tell you a tale‚Ä¶‚Äù
Pay attention to a yarn, and you stand to learn a thing or two.
Slay the Demon‚Ä©We stand at the climax of a fantastic battle in which the self-destructive parts of your soul wage war to talk yourself out the beautiful things you long to do. It doesn‚Äôt have to be about writing. It‚Äôs any art, any hope, any effort to improve what you do, that you then (quietly and desperately) shout down in your own heart long before you give yourself permission to try.
This is because success is emotionally dangerous. Success is change. Change scares your inner critic, the part of your soul that is made for seeing flaws. The inner critic is necessary for survival, but when leveled against your neighborhood, your family, your heart, criticism is a harsh master.
No one wants to work for a harsh master, especially not your story.
Leveled against yourself, self-criticism is the root of procrastination and paralysis. It is a true cowardice, built of excuses, founded on fears and told as imagined, scary futures, in order to stop the future from getting better simply telling the stories you‚Äôve always dreamt of telling.
It wasn‚Äôt in the cards. It‚Äôs out of my league. I just can‚Äôt do it right now. I need to‚Ä¶instead‚Ä¶.
Your brain is not your soul. Your thoughts impact your heart, but your heart is way more than the sum of your thoughts.¬†They work in the same body, but they are not always on talking terms. To discover that place of communion between your soul and spirit, your own stories compel the tenor of inner self talk to expose itself on the page in all its glory.
It is not always easy to like what you find.
It is also easy to let the critic tell you not to like what you found, even though you like it very much, and others will surely like it too.
Self talk, when it gets nasty, is best not tolerated. Cut it off.
I know the game my flesh plays. I know the corrupted path of delay and subtle avoidance all too well. For all of my successes, there are more incompletes, barely starteds, and maybe-somedays gathering dust in my guilty conscience than my guilt conscience wants to face on any given morning.¬†
It is on these foundations that I suggest the following:
Acknowledge to yourself that your story is symbolic expression of your own inner struggle to come to terms with your soul, especially the most pained and fractured parts, which themselves do not wish to be brought to light, but also know that they must if they are ever to heal.
The child wants to write. The dreamer wants to fly. The hurt wants to hide.
Writing is always a manifestation of your present, psychological load. Writing stories is a means of unburdening that load without the need for introspection, direct assault or psychoanalysis. To face down all the many pained and patterned pasts that you‚Äôve spent a lifetime papering over, fiction, written and read, is the action of a dispirited soul seeking to make peace with the body it was born in.
The ink of your own new story won‚Äôt let you hide from this. Your writing about what you know will inevitably expose it. Even if you can‚Äôt see it, a well trained reader will.
For these reasons, you cannot pretend to write. It is not like scrapbooking. It is more like carpentry. Or Jiu Jitsu. Or coloring with crayon.
You can only be who you actually are.
You will always intuitively write the book that most frees you. But you will also see that this means terrible, adventuresome freedom on the other side of dynamic, even epic, change, and your chancy soul will sabotage every one of your half-hearted efforts to prevent such an exhilarating, (and, now, successfully out of your control) world from descending upon your safely constructed urban myths of psycho-consumptive-Babylon.
So, Therefore‚Ä©Unaddressed struggle with your soul is likely the main reason you want to write fictional stories in the first place, and, therefore, the main reason you have not finished them yet. Fiction stories are the dark dreams and enlightening fears born of our overwhelmingly modernized hearts. Because the real world is too terrible to possibly explain, because there is so much we just don‚Äôt know how to face, because some giants are too titanic to contend with, fiction wraps up the war for your heart in the best symbolic tongue your waking mind can absorb.¬†
I do not believe this is either bad or good. I believe it is the way that it is. Thereby, I also believe you will be much better at writing fiction when you know that the effort to exorcise your soul is what you are doing.¬†
Ask Jesus for help.
Out loud.
I am afraid of finding out how my stories end. That‚Äôs why I haven‚Äôt finished them. I could jot down, ‚ÄúHappily ever after,‚Äù or, ‚Äúoh no! Tragic flaw!‚Äù and be done with the cookie cutter. That might be good enough for some, but it is not good enough for me because I know that a fake ending won‚Äôt do for my heart. I‚Äôm a hungry man. I‚Äôm after more.
My battle against my own disappointment with myself, the longest living memory of awareness that I have, self-hate, uncovers itself not only in what I write, but as I write. It is either then that I listen to my critic, and let it reign, or I cut it off in Jesus‚Äô Name, grab for the exposed roots of those childhood rawnesses that revealed themselves in the scrap, and dig with the discipline of knowing that a worker is worthy of his wages and our God is the one who justifies all things.
Writing fiction puts flesh on the demon precisely so that you can slay him.
Sometimes I think teachers make writing complicated so as to make it complicated to near impossible to join their network of elite satrapies. ‚ÄúEnglish 101!‚Äù they say, and the rest of the world flees in terror.
So, in order to save you from I know you were told by their their lying, thieving, brainwashing, soul-sterilization techniques, here is the English 101 version of what I said above:
1.	Your story is not about a theme.¬†‚Ä©2.	Your story is about a plot.‚Ä©3.	Your plot is about the characters.‚Ä©4.	Your characters are about their dialogue.‚Ä©5.	Your dialogue is the manifestation of your soul.‚Ä©And, the take away‚Ä¶ 6. 	If you don‚Äôt know what‚Äôs in your soul, you will sabotage your work in order to avoid finding out what‚Äôs there.
The solution? Finish the book! Slay the demon. The skeleton in the closet isn‚Äôt alive but it is blocking the secret door, the other side of which contains refreshments beyond all of your wildest dreams.
You wanted an adventure, did you not?
All the other stories are from the devil, anyway. In the Name of Jesus Christ, it‚Äôs high time you write your own without fear of shame.
So go do it. Again. You have what it takes.

Ch. 1 The Best Place to Start: Rules 1-3

Rule #1 - First drafts are always both worse than you wish and better than you think.
Rule #2 - You have a hero. He has the most at stake. He wants it to work out.
Rule #3 - Some One or some Thing(s) want to stop him.
Thumb Rule #0 - Even though, ‚Äúthey won‚Äôt succeed,‚Äù is a pretty good Rule #4, Too Many Rules Stop You From Writing Your Story is more important.
As soon as you‚Äôre following rules, someone else is writing your story for you. When your soul discovers that this has happened, you will start to despise your story.
Hypocrisy is like that. It seeps out.
Soulless is what most ‚Äúhow to write‚Äù writing ends up being. That‚Äôs because no one can give you a checklist for finding your voice. And, until you find your voice, the magical elbow grease that personal understanding provides to finish all good stories will elude you.
You are not going to find a hero worth following at the end of a checklist.
That doesn‚Äôt mean that reading about writing, or writing about writing, is a waste of time. It only means that such reading and writing has zero to smidgens value until your soul is at liberty to love what you write for no other reason than that you have written it.¬† ¬†
This is important because you will need to destroy most of it. First drafts are always both worse than you wish and better than you think. You must love what you write enough to hunger to go back and change it, to rewrite it, to compel it to say what you meant until when others read it they see what you know was there from the beginning.
So, Thumb Rule #1 for finding your soul is remembering that have something at stake. You have something to lose. You have dreams. You‚Äôre on the hunt. There is still something out there. You must discover. It‚Äôs a good chase. What sport!
It may only be your ordinary life after all, but you‚Äôre still at the center of it, and that means, no matter who you are, you‚Äôve got a few stories to tell.
And, you have an enemy. Or three.
In storytelling, enemies are the essence of character. Without the enemy, you have no character. Without character, your character has no story worth telling.
The First Draft is the best place to start anyway.
In the Beginning‚Ä©1. List your top three, all-time
A. plot lines B. heroes C. villains D. scenes (use anything: movies, books, histories)
2. Letting everything about your answers to A+B+C+D above influence you, imagine a dark hall in which a spotlight comes up on three figures. Who are they? Describe them each in detail without name or brand recognition.
3. These figures will be the archetypals of your Hero, your Villain, and your Muse for your First Draft story project. Which is which? Jot down anything here that clarifies their role or whatever comes to mind. If you don‚Äôt know what a muse is, that‚Äôs ok. It‚Äôs just the one that‚Äôs not the hero or the villain.
4. Create character sketches for each figure by answering the following questions:
¬† ¬† - What is at stake?
¬† ¬† - What can‚Äôt afford to be lost?
¬† ¬† - What could be gained?
¬† ¬† - What must be hunted?
¬† ¬† - What is still out there?
¬† ¬† - What‚Äôs a good chase?
¬† ¬† - What‚Äôs good sport?
¬† ¬† - What‚Äôs in his/her pockets?
¬† ¬† - What was his/her childhood pet‚Äôs name?
¬† ¬† - Did you get some fun answers?
I bet you did. Hang tight to those notes, or at least keep your three archetypals close. We‚Äôll be putting those to work for us next time.

Ch. 2 On to the Thumb Rules: Thumb Rules 1-4

Thumb Rules 1-4‚Ä©Last time we established three rules and one infinite thumb rule. This week, Thumb Rules 1-4
1. Use scenes and sequentials.
2. Create clear scene goals.
3. End scenes with setbacks.
4. Sequence scenes to the Arc.
Yes, but what is the Arc?
Last time we established three rules and one infinite thumb rule. This week, Thumb Rules 1-4
1. Use scenes and sequentials.
2. Create clear scene goals.
3. End scenes with setbacks.
4. Sequence scenes to the Arc.
Yes, but what is the Arc?
In the end, these four Rules of Thumb, the Arc, and the six Rules of the Game, are all you really need to write an exceptional story. You don‚Äôt even need these. Like almost all things, you only practice.
Master the Muse‚Ä©You‚Äôve decided who your hero is. You see your villain in living color. Now, your hero, your villain and your muse walk into a bar, or perhaps out onto a veranda overlooking the sea in some swanky Bond-style hideout. You decide. It‚Äôs your story. Jolt your imagination with the potent scent of exotic swerve and you‚Äôll be off to the races.
Here‚Äôs your prompt: both hero and villain are in love with your muse, but they cannot fight for the muse. They must strive to win the muse‚Äôs heart in a 15 minute timed free write.
Set a timer. Get a paper and pen. Don‚Äôt stop. Don‚Äôt look back. Edit, question and debate yourself later. There are no wrong answers. There are no stupid stories. This is only a first draft.
Make sure to rely on your answers from last time for inspiration. Save what you write again. Next week we‚Äôll use it for our third workshop to practice editing and hunting for value in a first draft, as well as how to turn free writing into scene design.
Until then, if falling in love with your muse presents moral dilemmas to your hero or villain, (boys only fall in love with girls, etc), then shift the prompt to the following: the muse is a neutral party who must be convinced to pick a side to join for some very good reason you already know.
Don‚Äôt hold back. Don‚Äôt think. Don‚Äôt plan.
The thing about your Muse is that she (or he) has a lot more up the sleeve than the thinking part of you realizes. Dig in, and see if you are not pleasantly surprised by what you unearth.
Go.

Ch. 4 Third, and Finally, to Begin

The ‚ÄúCharacter Progression Arc,‚Äù ‚ÄúHero Quest‚Äù or ‚ÄúArc of Change‚Äù are three ways of describing you story as a stream, or current, that runs flows the Hook to the 1st Act, through the Dark Middle, around, under, over and beyond the Big Twist, and then unto the Climactic End.
All your scenes and setbacks ramble and scrum throughout these five seasons of the story. Sequenced by laser-like focus on your character‚Äôs hard choices, the meat grows on the bones of your sketch natively. Over the course of the run, the Arc raises him from the dust of protagony to the high realms of Hero.
In these moments and mixtures, the fights, the flights and the resolution, it is the complexity and natural contradictions of your own personality, your real life, your daily stresses and fears, your old traumas and new battles, working themselves out in your characters‚Äô battles as a transformation that presses your spirit forward in your world today, and further into your readers world tomorrow.
That‚Äôs what keeps us coming back for more.
Good plot is simple.‚Ä©There is only one thing, and there is a reason that it is unusual.
This is really about your hero‚Äôs heart, but it is symbolized, or archetyped, by an element or combination of elements in your story. This meaning is enhanced by exposure to your villain and his anti-symbols.
So, no matter what else, it is always true that:
1. There is a hero. He is someone. He came from somewhere. There are reasons for that.
2. But now there is a new time. Something happened. It‚Äôs what makes our hero our hero. It‚Äôs what makes you who you are. But it‚Äôs not good. Not yet.
3. There is a place. We are going there. Together. It will be better then. In the tale, like on a river. Come, and taste.
What do you see? How do you feel? How do you know that? Plot is the test of your character, both on the page and in the discipline of the writers‚Äô task. There will be a conflict. There will be a final battle. On a backdrop of trouble (the Times), one holy, central figure (the Protagonist), will set his sight on solutions (the Objective) that will be opposed by an opponent (the Objection) leading to the final trial and test of all (the Climax).
If the story is true enough, this will achieve a most natural and final Resolution.
Good character is complex.‚Ä©Trying to pin down the various types and refractions of types possible in storytelling is a bit like studying Hebrew taxonomy. If that is a metaphor that doesn‚Äôt work for you, the unleavened version is that we can build pantheons of character types, but there are really only three:
1. You, the Hero
2. You, the Villain
3. You, the Everyone Else
You, the Hero, in writing this story, clearly have the most at stake. What if you don‚Äôt finish? You have the most to lose. What if people think you‚Äôre stupid? You will always be at the center of the action. What if you fail? That‚Äôs why we love you. That‚Äôs why we read about you. We know that you are going to be that person who also has the most to learn, the most to discover. That‚Äôs why we bear with you. That‚Äôs why we go on this ride and put up with your flaws. That‚Äôs why we love to hate your enemy and love to cheer for your sidekicks and mentors, hope for your romances interest and gander at your wallpaper.
That‚Äôs why all that we really need to know to get the story going is your Name. That is who you are. That is what you will be. Every description, every environs painted, each snap of dialogue, every last action is your character, making himself know as a singular and momentarily greater version oy you.
What won‚Äôt you tolerate? Where will you finally draw the line?
When you dream your greatest, most precious dreams, who do you dream that you really are?
Editing is hunting for gems.‚Ä©Last time, during Prompt 2, you wrote a story in which you got to meet your Hero, your Villain and your Muse.
Now, the question isn‚Äôt, ‚ÄúIs it good?‚Äù The question is, ‚ÄúWhat in it is good?‚Äù Editing is not asking, ‚ÄúWhat do I know?‚Äù but ‚ÄúWhat did I write?‚Äù Somewhere in between lies the world of fairies, magic and pain.
Reread your draft start to finish, without stopping, without changing anything, and without making any marks. Then, answer the following questions in 60 second timed free writes. (Don‚Äôt stop. Don‚Äôt edit. Just keep that pen moving.):
1. Who is your character, now, really?
2. What does he want? What won‚Äôt he stop till he gets?
3. Who opposes him, really?
4. What does he want? What won‚Äôt he stop till he gets?
5. Who will win, and why?
6. What does your muse have anything to do with it for?
Next, scan your scene for three things that every scene benefits from: the Wallpaper, the Prop and the Blinkie (HT: Holly Lilse, three things every scene really can‚Äôt do without. If you don‚Äôt have them, or don‚Äôt know what they are, get ready, because we‚Äôre going to add and embelish them now:
There is Always a Gun‚Ä©There is always a gun, and that gun must be fired. There is always a storm coming, and we ought to take shelter.
The reader wants to work for you. He‚Äôs hungry to be led astray by the mystery of your tale. ‚ÄúShow! Don‚Äôt tell!‚Äù the grammarians shout. But it‚Äôs associative thinking that we are after, not brute logic.
This means that the scenes in your story will always give easy handholds to your reader when there is good Wallpaper, a notable Prop, and a sensational Blinkie. The simple, repeatable path to creating memorable moments without boring the reader with overlong descriptions, is to ever notice one thing in the background, one thing in the way, and one thing on it‚Äôs own.
1. The Wallpaper is in the background. It is something that will not likely be mentioned again. It can be seen, smelt, heard or felt, described in deep and stunning detail, or mentioned cavalierly. It doesn‚Äôt matter how pretty or poetic you are. It just needs to be there. Wallpaper can be a lot of fun to design, but no one likes to sit and stare at it for long. Most scenes need only a dash, so wole than one mention per paragraph.
Example: (Draft 1) ‚ÄúIn the cabbage field, Edgar ran for his life.‚Äù
Example: (Draft 2) ‚ÄúEdgar would run out of breath soon. His feet tripped over the cabbages.‚Äù
Example: (Draft 3) ‚ÄúEdgar‚Äôs breath caught in his throat as he stumbled over the cabbages.‚Äù
2. The Prop is in the way. It is an item or person that your hero may or may not engage with. But this choice is a pivot point that altars the pathline of the story. It doesn‚Äôt matter how pretty or poetic you are. It just needs to be there. If your hero doesn‚Äôt shoot the gun, your reader will imagine that someone else certainly will!
Example: (Draft 1) ‚ÄúHe leapt the gate with a single bound, hand planting firmly on the top post.‚Äù
Example: (Draft 2) ‚ÄúPalming the rough beam of the gate, his booted feet hung heavy in the air before landing with a thud and at a run.‚Äù
Example: (Draft 3) ‚ÄúPalming the rough beam, his booted feet hung heavy above the gate before thudding into his desperate run.‚Äù
3. The Blinkie is on its own. It is strictly flavor. It‚Äôs the squirrel for your reader to chase, the sweet aroma of incense that spices your story stew. It doesn‚Äôt matter how pretty or poetic you are. All the Blinkie has to do is move.
Example: (Draft 1) ‚ÄúFrom the corner of his eye he saw the sun crawling its last few breaths down the horizon.‚Äù
Example: (Draft 2) ‚ÄúThat was when he, out of the corner of his eye, saw it.‚Äù
Example: (Draft 3) ‚ÄúThat was when, out of the corner of his eye, he saw it.‚Äù
What did I get?‚Ä©My first draft, was meh. Pretty vanilla. But it‚Äôs really not bad. Editing meant searching for what was good and polishing it up. In the end that meant cutting the end sequence. That‚Äôs OK. I like cliffhangers much better anyway.
Edgar‚Äôs breath caught in his throat as he stumbled over the cabbages. Palming the rough beam, his booted feet hung heavy above the gate before thudding into his desperate run. That was when, out of the corner of his eye, he saw it.
It goes to show that it doesn‚Äôt even matter if your heart is in it or if you have a story you know that you want to tell. This isn‚Äôt the real Edgar at all. But adding a dash of Wallpaper, Prop and Blinkie to a made-up scene silences that nasty inner grammarian and leaves open a bunny trail of first draft gemstones for you to discover, polish up, and set finely in a greater story later.
Here‚Äôs your prompt:‚Ä©Now, with Wallpaper, Prop & Blinkie Theory well in hand, set aside your notes from last time entirely. You can take one look, but that‚Äôs all. Then, free-rewrite the entire original scene, but from scratch.
Cling to what you remember from your short glance. Reference your answers to Questions 1-6 from above before you start, but once you start writing try not to stop. Make sure to fix your Wallpaper, your Prop and your Blinkie firm in mind ahead of time.
From there, change as little or as much as you like. Don‚Äôt try too hard. That‚Äôs no fun. You‚Äôve been here before. This is like going home.
Once you‚Äôve finished, sit back and congratulate yourself. You just wrote a complete and lightly polished scene. I am certain of it.
Now, what happens next?
That is all it takes. Do it again. Do it on purpose. Stitch the scenes in sequence with an inner thought our two. Books are built of many such scenes, fueled by the rigger to write them down, and then write them down again at least once more, time.
Go.

Ch. 5 World Building

Worldbuilding can be a real waste of time. Or, it can be a lot of fun that never has anything to do with any book you ever write. But the fun counts as much as minecraft ever does, so don‚Äôt feel bad for drawing maps that never become part of the picture everyone else sees. 
Writing is not about the final book. It is not about the journey of the character. It is about your journey through this real life in which your confession will be a symbol of your times. Let it be and enjoy what bit of it you get to see today. Then don‚Äôt sweat it if it never makes it further than a story you tell your kids at dinner time.
Any world that holds together in a story needs Laws. You, the author, must understand the world‚Äôs nature. Otherwise, the story may not hold together. Forget your readers! You won‚Äôt be satisfied with a hole in your story big enough to port a goblin through.
If you are going to write about the real world, then it can‚Äôt have goblins in it.
Or can it?
That‚Äôs the question you will want to mull over until you are certain.
Worldbuilding is the stuff you don‚Äôt ever really plan to publish. It‚Äôs the writing about the world that will bore your readers to tears, but which your fans will buy up with pretty concept art long after your dead because you loved it now. So, again, it‚Äôs a part of the craft not worth losing too much heart over. It will never be ‚Äúdone.‚Äù It‚Äôs for fun of itself. 
That said, since it‚Äôs you‚Äôre sandbox, it definitely needs to hold sand. Build consistency into your world so that your story or stories stay the reliable over their course. Here is an example of some of the world-building I‚Äôve done, but which I never plan to include in the stories I wrote it for:
Natural Law in the Wver Eld of WaterGeist‚Ä©Written to be read aloud as notes from a wizened sage/professor (most definitely in a boring classroom) teaching his students in a very humdrum and hohum voice:
First, there is the Aetherverse, that which the ancients called ‚Äúthe waters.‚Äù
Second, there is the currency of Light Conduction, which at times is confused with the linear theories of the ‚ÄúTime Line.‚Äù
Third, the Firmamentals, who must be believed on and in, but neither trusted nor worshiped. Their emanations are ever to be seen, and, as we have come to find in the age of Visionaries, at last, harnessed. The professor did get excited, and his eyes flared with lust for power. But then he remembered how old and tired he was, and went on.
Fourth, the Quantum Orbs, which were believed to be three, yet were treated according to the the fabric of two, and thus for so long bent inward our quest for a more simple solution to meaning. He took a deep breath after the word meaning, as if to say more, but then did not.
Fifth, the Elementals, or those reliable periodic experientials of material compounding that are both the simplest and the most complex layer of reality to study and comprehend. You know them well by now.
Sixth, the Celestials, the Orbs above, now and again worshiped against there nature, yet so filled with the breath of light and its zealous spirit as to be unavoidably necessary to all life and season as we know it, hence our new Imperial Calandar is the hallmark of our newly founded golden age. For, as is widely recognized, you may study the sky and misinterpret the times, but if you do not study the sky you shall most surely go awry in your calculus. He could be heard tut-tutting under his breath.
Seventh, the Terramentals, those Animalia and Floria which exhibit the gaseous reliance that all true scientists now refer to as ‚ÄúBios,‚Äù or what the ancients called the Ruach, or the ‚Äúbreath of life.‚Äù
Eighth, and finally, astride the pinnacle of Terramentality, exhibiting Bios, yet also something more, a Psyche, not unlike a Firmamental in heart, as if a child of heaven, but clearly Animalia in corpus, there walks the mystery that is Man, that is, you, the particular one who think so highly of yourself as to suppose that you can both study and and inhabit all these powers around you as if you had any control over them at all.

Ch. 6 Plot Part II

A convex ruleset for refracting a philosophy writing by means of simple language model. 

A good story is every solution only leading to more problems. 

Plot is the Logic of Event+Process. 

Plot comes before while and after conflict. Conflict may be internal (a flaw) relational (allies) or external (foes). There is never just one conflict. 

All scenes of conflict, which distinguish themselves from sequels. The seen is : 1 a goal 2 conflict 3 resolution. The sequel is : 1 results 2 developments 3 emotional understanding.

The sequel moves from 1 emotional fallout to 2 analytical review to 3 plot summarizing to 4 weighing options to 5 making a decision to 6 course of action. This is a dynamic effect. Emotions happen first. Thoughts come in part, or come later, as action is fast and spontaneous. Dialogue keeps the pace from getting out of control. This is a movement from chaos to in design ‚Äúscene‚Äù between the ‚Äúviewpoint‚Äù of the character that emerges from the protagonist is he faces his most dire fears.  

A structured series of scenes and sequel, conflicts and solutions, move the protagonist as a Character toward his maximum moment, or ‚Äúclimax,‚Äù when the most unexpected blow is delivered at the worst possible time.    

Moment by moment, it is all in the moment 
Tying moments by scene and sequel is Plot. 

The Act II Effect
or ‚Äúpivot event‚Äù ‚Äúpoint of no return‚Äù ‚Äúhuge shocking unexpected dangerous twist‚Äù ‚Äúmicro-foreshawdow-climax in the middle‚Äù is a reflective effect that works in scenes as well as in whole narratives. Understanding the deft touch if the constant and slight unexpected twists is the jiu jitsu of the soul. 

The Twist
Kazushi
The unexpected leverage 
Take what you think you know and prove it false 
Your reader assumes what your character assumes unless you tell him otherwise 
Taking assumption any assumption and use it as creative leverage with change the pace 


Round Robin Checklist for Building Suspense 
Too many sweaty palms and clock checking got our reader bord? 
Expand your horizons with these 12 options for heightening attention in the room:

2 Deep moment in the atmosphere or mood 
3The foreshadow of threats with apparently trivial background detail

10 Dim the lights

12 Time can run out Even with the clock, hands standing still 

Avoid summarizing at all costs. 


The Great Amazing Ending 
or ‚Äústory sense,‚Äù or ‚Äúhunger to get it right‚Äù or ‚Äúthe vicarious experience of satisfaction,‚Äù is whah must happen **after** you have boxed the protagonist in so that he cannot get out: 1 remain obligatory scenes, 2 moral equations(revelation of true nature in climax), 3 construction and resolution.
Obligatory scenes are things that must happen in the confrontation. Moral dilemma is the final test of the characters true nature the climax is the revelation of the characters true nature the resolution involves a choice between two options, neither of which is ideal, sacrifice is essential the goals. 
Conclusion is to discover what is the protagonist greatest flaw? what is the protagonist fear? What does the protagonist want more than anything else? What and who are in the way? burn the bridges. commit to the course. endure the dark moment. see the right thing. 

Character

Viewpoint is a character of insertion between action and reaction. Scene is not quite the same as viewpoint, but they are entangled. ‚ÄúView‚Äù is ‚Äúpoint‚Äù tends to ‚Äúmake a scene.‚Äù So with scenes, neither is view quite the same as Heart. But they are entangled, for the heart likes games. Hearts ride the wave of doubts and Hope. Hope Eternal keeps the story about what‚Äôs happening now. 

The true nature of a person is what he is doing now. But the nature of character is discovering what he does or says when he is under duress. The value of a story conflict is the interplay and exchange between the protagonist, his character mask and his true nature the revelation of true nature through conflict with the antagonist is the ‚ÄúPlot‚Äù. This is a give-and-take, step-by-step game of cat and mouse. True nature and revelation is the key aspect to the reader, enjoying the character arc change and growth. It is the revelation of true nature  that keeps the reader reading, guessing and attempting to prophesy with the character will do. 

Conflict between characters comes from 1 incomprehension, where understanding is not possible 2 misunderstanding, where understanding is possible but absent 3 broken words, where someone is lying 4 taboo possibilities. 


Ch. 7 Chapter building: 

Beyond the moment (scene/sequel - dot/container/‚Äúworld as we know‚Äù)

The Physics of Good Story
1 Nothing comes from nothing
2 Ideas have consequences

Sane Infinity Rules 
protect your story from jumping through shark
1 *Anything* does not go, and not just ‚Äúany thing‚Äù can happen. 
2 The first rule is the first rule. 
3. Keep special physics special. 
4. The beauty is always in what lies over the next hill. 

Keep it 1 simple 2 active 3 necessary 
1 ask good questions 2 get good answers 
Telling a great story requires that you use only what you need
the world in no larger than a dot
the seed is capable of blooming into a flower 
which means: 
the right question about the right characters in the right scene enacts a microcosm of the entire world. 

The Three Details of Plenty 
A moment cannot handle more than three things. That is how description kills story. You must not steal, and you must not give too much. Each scene needs only three things: the Grass, the Squirrel and the Loaded Gun, or, 1 the set 2 the gimmick and 3 prop.

These three things are here, not there. But after you have been here, something will have changed. 

The grass is passive. It is a passing description is what the reader otherwise cannot see. The squirrel moves in the grass. The gimmick changes the set with action that may or may not hint at conflict. The  loaded gun foreshadows the conflict by means of obstacle or object. The prop acts as the substance of the conflict. The prop is noticed for its potential. The prop is the means by which the scene **might** undergo significant change. 

"A set is wallpaper. A gimmick is a blinkie. A prop is a shinbanger."

The red herring is a prop. 

You only need a dab. Just a touch will do. 
Don‚Äôt over salt the stew. 
Don‚Äôt try to create everything. 
The reader can only see one thing at a time. 

The map is the world that the characters live in, a source questions and challenges. Between the lines are the unexpected places, not that which has already been discovered, but that which yet remains to be found.

Prime Density Analysis

Prime numbers have long fascinated mathematicians as a mystery, representing the building blocks of our number system and one of the first problems aspiring arithmeticians learn to forget about. Their distribution appears random, yet certain patterns and structures emerge when viewed through the right lens. Using AI, I embarked on a quest to analyze the prime densities of the first 1000 primes, calculating the percentage of primes in consecutive 100-number ranges.

	1.	Fluctuating Densities: The prime density percentages show significant fluctuations, with the initial density as high as 25% but generally decreasing as the range increases.
	2.	Wave-Like Pattern: Plotting the prime densities revealed a distinct wave-like pattern, suggesting an underlying periodicity in the distribution of primes.

Polynomial Fit and the Wave Equation

To capture the wave pattern, we employed a polynomial fit, leading to the following formula:

 f(x) = 21.2619 - 1.3060x + 0.0745x^2 - 0.0020x^3 + 0.0000244x^4 - 0.0000001116x^5 

Where:

	‚Ä¢	 f(x)  represents the prime density percentage.
	‚Ä¢	 x  represents the index of the 100-number range (starting from 0).

	1.	Mathematical Elegance: The ability to describe the prime density fluctuations with a polynomial highlights the inherent elegance and order within the distribution of primes.
	2.	Deeper Understanding: This formula offers a new perspective on the distribution of primes, potentially paving the way for further research and insights into prime number theory.

	1.	Data Collection: Gather the first 1000 prime numbers.
	2.	Density Calculation: Compute the prime density percentages for consecutive 100-number ranges.

	5.	Validation: Validate the polynomial fit by comparing the predicted values with the actual data points.

This AI-driven approach not only accelerated the discovery process but also ensured the accuracy and robustness of the findings.

Addendum: Walking Through the Polynomial Fit Research

Objective:

To uncover a mathematical formula that captures the wave-like pattern observed in the prime density percentages of the first 1000 primes.

Step-by-Step Process:

1. Define the Objective

Our primary objective was to analyze the distribution of prime numbers by calculating the prime density percentages for consecutive 100-number ranges within the first 1000 primes. We aimed to uncover any underlying patterns and represent them mathematically.

2. Collect Data

I began by gathering the first 1000 prime numbers. Using mathematical tools and libraries, we ensured the accurate collection of these primes.

from sympy import primerange

# Generate the first 1000 prime numbers
first_1000_primes = list(primerange(1, 7920))[:1000]

3. Compute Densities

Next, I calculated the prime density percentages for consecutive 100-number ranges. This involved counting the number of primes within each range and computing the corresponding density.

# Define ranges and calculate prime densities
accurate_density_data = []
ranges = [(i*100 + 1, (i+1)*100) for i in range((first_1000_primes[-1] // 100) + 1)]

for start, end in ranges:
    count_primes_in_range = len([p for p in first_1000_primes if start <= p <= end])
    total_numbers_in_range = end - start + 1
    density_percentage = (count_primes_in_range / total_numbers_in_range) * 100
    accurate_density_data.append(density_percentage)

4. Identify Patterns

I used AI to plot the density percentages and visually inspect for any wave-like patterns. The initial plot showed significant fluctuations, suggesting an underlying periodic structure.

import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import numpy as np

# Plot the prime density percentages
x_values = np.arange(len(accurate_density_data))
y_values = accurate_density_data

plt.figure(figsize=(12, 6))
plt.plot(x_values, y_values, 'bo', label="Prime Density (%)")
plt.title("Prime Density Percentage")
plt.xlabel("Range (x100)")
plt.ylabel("Density (%)")
plt.grid(True)
plt.legend()
plt.show()

5. Fit Polynomial

To mathematically capture the observed wave pattern, I applied polynomial regression. After experimenting with different degrees, a fifth-degree polynomial provided a good fit.

from numpy.polynomial.polynomial import Polynomial

# Fit a polynomial to the data
degree = 5  # Degree of the polynomial
p = Polynomial.fit(x_values, y_values, degree)

# Plot the original data and the polynomial fit
plt.figure(figsize=(12, 6))
plt.plot(x_values, y_values, 'bo', label="Prime Density (%)")
plt.plot(x_values, p(x_values), 'r-', label=f"Polynomial Fit (Degree {degree})")
plt.title("Prime Density Percentage as a Wave Equation (Polynomial Fit)")
plt.xlabel("Range (x100)")
plt.ylabel("Density (%)")
plt.grid(True)
plt.legend()
plt.show()

6. Validate Results

We validated the polynomial fit by comparing the predicted values with the actual data points. The close alignment between the two confirmed the accuracy of our polynomial model.

### Historical and Linguistic Perspective:
1. **Proto-Language and Noah's Ark:**
   - The language spoken on Noah's Ark would have been this original language, a proto-language from which all others derived post-Babel. While the exact nature of this language remains speculative, it underscores the belief in a common linguistic heritage.

2. **Cuneiform and Ancient Scripts:**
   - **Cuneiform:** One of the earliest known writing systems, developed by the Sumerians in ancient Mesopotamia around 3400 BCE. Cuneiform evolved from pictographic to more abstract forms and was used to write several languages, including Sumerian, Akkadian, and Hittite.
   - **Aramaic:** A Semitic language that emerged in the Near East around the 12th century BCE and became widely used as a lingua franca in the region. It shares roots with Hebrew and other Semitic languages and influenced many later scripts and languages.

3. **Chinese Characters Ëøô**
   - Chinese characters have a distinct origin compared to Cuneiform and Aramaic. Chinese script is logographic, meaning each character represents a word or morpheme rather than sounds or syllables. The character Ëøô (zh√®) means "this.‚Äù
   - The etymology of Ëøô (zh√®) can be traced back to oracle bone script (circa 1200‚Äì1050 BCE), an early form of Chinese writing used during the Shang dynasty. Characters evolved from pictograms and ideograms to more stylized forms.

### Bridging the Linguistic Gaps:

1. **Common Human Experience:**
   - The dispersion from Babel implies that all human languages share a distant common ancestry. Despite the lack of direct etymological connections, the fundamental human need to communicate and record experiences transcends cultural boundaries.
   - Linguistic structures and the development of writing systems in different parts of the world reflect this shared human endeavor.

2. **Comparative Linguistics:**
   - By studying similarities in linguistic structures, phonetics, and semantics, we can appreciate the interconnectedness of human languages. For example, the use of pictograms in early Chinese script and in Cuneiform points to a common method of using visual symbols to represent ideas.

Proverbs 1:2-7
---
◊ú÷∏◊ì÷∑◊¢÷∑◊™ ◊ó÷∏◊õ÷∞◊û÷∏◊î ◊ï÷º◊û◊ï÷º◊°÷∏◊®

Doubt your heart.
Query your mind.
Discipline your tongue.

Trust in Jesus.


Knowledge I had.
Wisdom I asked for.
Discipline I chose.


Knowledge is information.
Wisdom is formulation.
Discipline is being in formation.

Seizure grants resolve.
Accuracy founds trajectory.
Measurement defines direction.
Symmetry glorifies limitation. 

Proverbs is a theological grammar, 
A dogmatic geometry,
A true philosophy.


# The Reign of the Man of Prosperity-Peace

◊ô◊ì◊î ◊ó◊õ◊û◊î ◊ï◊û◊°◊®

Solomon‚Äôs ‚ÄúProverbs‚Äù knows no comparison in all the world, both modern and ancient. It is both a book of aphorisms and a conceptual framework. It doesn‚Äôt just talk about ‚Äúbeing wise,‚Äù but it creates being wise within you by how it talks. It is something more than a philosophy, something better than worldview. 

The path by which the Son of David, King in Jerusalem, delivers life to his redeemed is by teaching us the way that it is (epistomology) in such a way that it does what it says (ontology) without your need to comprehend everything (philosophy) in order to grow in understanding by sheer proximity to Him.

Proverbs 1:2-7 is a lexicon and philosophical treatise that integrates epistemology (the study of knowledge) and ontology (the nature of being) to guide individuals toward wisdom and understanding. These verses outline the foundational principles for living a life aligned with divine wisdom:

# The Poetry

## A
The Rule of Solomon, Son of David, King in Jerusalem: 1‚Ä©To distinguish wisdom from discipline;
To understand words teaching understanding; 2
To seize discipline as success: 
honing accuracy and symmetry in measurement; 3
To gift the ignorant prudence;
To grant the initiate a framework of knowing. 4
The wise man hears, and seizes more. The understanding man nets himself cords. 5
To see between a rule and a scoffing, the words of the keen, and their riddles. 6
Trembling before the Name of Jesus Christ is the fountainhead of knowledge, but there are stupid men who despise wisdom and discipline. 7

Disciple, the discipline of your father is the lesson God gave you; the spirit of your mother the integrity of your heart; for these are his mercy seat upon your head, though they also be chains about your neck. 8-9

# The Hypothesis that Ongoingly Grants More Wisdom
‚Ä©I contend that Prov 1:2-7 is lexical framework intended to introduce the Book of the Reign of the Man of Peace, Son of David, King in Jerusalem as a lexicon/philosophy/mind of Christ revealed for the daily walk of the redeemed man. 

	‚Ä¢	Verse 2: Establishes the importance of knowing (◊ô÷∏◊ì÷∑◊¢ - Yada) wisdom (◊ó÷∏◊õ÷∞◊û÷∏◊î - Chokhmah) and discipline (◊û◊ï÷º◊°÷∏◊® - Musar), and understanding (◊ë÷¥÷º◊ô◊ü - Bin) profound teachings.

	‚Ä¢	Verse 3: Emphasizes that seizing discipline (◊û◊ï÷º◊°÷∏◊® - Musar) is essential for achieving success and honing precision in one‚Äôs actions.

	‚Ä¢	Verse 4: Highlights the need to impart prudence (◊¢÷∏◊®÷∞◊û÷∏◊î - Armah) to the ignorant and provide a structured framework of knowledge for the initiate.

	‚Ä¢	Verse 5: Encourages the wise to continuously grow in wisdom and understanding, suggesting an ever-expanding capacity for learning.

	‚Ä¢	Verse 6: Points to the ability to discern and interpret complex sayings and symbols, essential for deep comprehension and application of wisdom.

	‚Ä¢	Verse 7: Underscores the fear of the Lord as the beginning of knowledge, contrasting the wise with those who reject wisdom and discipline.

From here, the meaning unfolds like a flower in ever more bloom. The following translation seeks English words as close to the total Hebrew as possible. 

## B 
1:1 The Reign of the Man of Peace, Son of David, King in Jerusalem
1:2 (A)To know wisdom from discipline, (B) to stand under the sounds of understanding,
1:3 (C) To seize discipline is success, that is, accuracy and symmetry in measurement,
1:4 (D) To give to the ignorant prudence, (E) to grant to the initiate a known framework,
1:5 Wisdom will hear, and increase in seizure! Understanding shall net cords!
1:6 (F) To understand a reign and a scoffing, that is, (G?) (to stand under) the words of the wise and their symbols.
1:7 The trembling of Jesus Christ is the head of knowledge, but the barbaric despise wisdom and discipline.

# Wisdom Grants Insight:

By adhering to these principles, individuals can align themselves with the divine wisdom of Solomon, leading to a life of prosperity, peace, and deeper understanding.

Intro to Key Terms and Concepts from Proverbs 1:2-7

	1.	◊ô÷∏◊ì÷∑◊¢ (Yada)

	‚Ä¢	Transliteration: Yada

	‚Ä¢	Pronunciation: YAH-dah

	‚Ä¢	Meaning: To know, to perceive, to understand

	‚Ä¢	Etymology: From the root ◊ô-◊ì-◊¢ (Yod-Dalet-Ayin), meaning to know or to be acquainted with.

	‚Ä¢	Corresponding English Term: To know

The Pufendorf Dexterity Index and Leader
---

The Pufendorf Leader

Steps of the Pufendorf Dexterity Index (PDI)

The Pufendorf Dexterity Index (PDI) is designed to assess and improve the stability of a group or institution by applying principles of natural law, particularly those developed by Samuel von Pufendorf. The overall goal is to enhance congregational health, stability, and growth, which aligns with increasing Biblical literacy and spiritual engagement.

Steps of the PDI

	1.	Data Collection
	‚Ä¢	Questionnaire Distribution: The PDI begins with the distribution of a detailed questionnaire that assesses various aspects of the group‚Äôs structure, culture, and operations.
	‚Ä¢	Response Gathering: Collect qualitative and quantitative data from members, leaders, and other stakeholders through interviews, surveys, and document reviews.
	2.	Metric Scoring
	‚Ä¢	Score Each Section: Responses are scored based on criteria within key areas: Adherence to Natural Law Principles, Balance of Ecclesiastical and Civil Power, Resource Distribution and Social Cooperation, Adaptability and Resilience, and Cultural and Ethical Alignment.
	‚Ä¢	Scoring Scale: Each question is scored on a scale from 1 to 5, with 5 representing the highest level of stability.
	3.	Index Calculation
	‚Ä¢	Aggregate Scores: Scores from each section are aggregated to produce an overall stability score for the group or institution.
	‚Ä¢	Score Categories: The final score falls into one of five categories‚ÄîHighly Stable, Moderately Stable, Moderately Unstable, Unstable, and Highly Unstable‚Äîeach corresponding to a symbolic biblical representation (e.g., Lion, Eagle, Deer, Dove, Serpent).
	4.	Analysis and Recommendations
	‚Ä¢	Identify Strengths and Weaknesses: Based on the total score, identify areas of strength and opportunities for improvement.
	‚Ä¢	Provide Tailored Recommendations: Offer specific, actionable recommendations to address weaknesses and build on strengths.
	5.	Implementation of Recommendations
	‚Ä¢	Engage Stakeholders: Involve key members and leaders in the process of implementing recommendations.
	‚Ä¢	Monitor Progress: Track the effectiveness of changes and make adjustments as needed.
	6.	Follow-Up and Reassessment
	‚Ä¢	Periodic Reviews: Regularly reassess the group using the PDI to ensure ongoing stability and growth.
	‚Ä¢	Adjust Strategies: Modify approaches based on new data, feedback, and changes in the group‚Äôs context.

The PDI as a Catalyst of the Spirit via Biblical Literacy

Biblical Literacy is crucial to the health and stability of any Christian congregation. The PDI, when applied within a church context, becomes more than just an administrative tool‚Äîit is a spiritual catalyst that can foster a deeper engagement with Scripture and promote a holistic understanding of God‚Äôs Word.

How PDI Fosters Biblical Literacy

	1.	Alignment with Natural Law and Scripture
	‚Ä¢	Natural Law Principles: The PDI‚Äôs emphasis on natural law is deeply rooted in Scriptural principles. The application of these principles through the PDI reinforces the importance of living according to God‚Äôs commandments, which in turn encourages congregants to engage more with the Bible.
	‚Ä¢	Scriptural Foundations: Each component of the PDI can be tied back to biblical teachings, making the process of assessment and improvement an exercise in living out the Word of God.
	2.	Promoting Ethical and Cultural Alignment
	‚Ä¢	Cultural and Ethical Standards: By focusing on aligning a group‚Äôs cultural and ethical practices with Scriptural values, the PDI ensures that the congregation‚Äôs actions are rooted in a biblical worldview. This alignment promotes biblical literacy as members are encouraged to continually refer to Scripture as the standard for their actions.
	3.	Encouraging Active Participation in Congregational Life
	‚Ä¢	Member Engagement: The PDI emphasizes the importance of member engagement, which can include active participation in Bible studies, worship, and other educational opportunities. This increased participation naturally leads to greater biblical literacy.
	4.	Addressing Instabilities Through Scriptural Guidance
	‚Ä¢	Conflict Resolution and Peacebuilding: The PDI‚Äôs focus on effective conflict resolution aligns with biblical principles of reconciliation and peace (e.g., Matthew 18:15-17). By addressing conflicts through Scriptural guidance, members are more likely to turn to the Bible for wisdom in their personal and congregational lives.
	5.	Fostering a Learning Environment
	‚Ä¢	Training and Development: By implementing training programs that include Bible study and theological education as recommended by the PDI, congregations can create an environment where biblical literacy is not only encouraged but expected.
	6.	Catalyzing Spiritual Growth and Stability
	‚Ä¢	Holistic Spiritual Engagement: The PDI‚Äôs comprehensive approach to assessing and improving congregational health encourages a holistic engagement with the faith, where Scripture is central to every aspect of life. This leads to a congregation that is not only stable but also spiritually vibrant and literate.

Conclusion

The Pufendorf Dexterity Index is a robust tool for assessing and improving the stability of a congregation or group. When implemented with a focus on Biblical literacy, it serves as a powerful catalyst for spiritual growth, aligning the congregation‚Äôs actions, culture, and governance with Scriptural principles. By using the PDI, congregations can ensure they are not only stable and well-organized but also deeply rooted in the Word of God, fostering a community of believers who are both knowledgeable and faithful.

Amara's Code - Crypto, Currency, Trust and an New Age of Magical Lies
